Instead, the Court appears to take the position that exercising essentially any executive power whatsoever is enough.  <em>Ante</em>, at 27-29.  In terms of explanation, the Court says that it is "not well-suited to weigh the relative importance of the regulatory and enforcement authority of disparate agencies" and that it "do[es] not think that the constitutionality of removal restrictions hinges on such an inquiry."  <em>Ante</em>, at 29.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's position unduly encroaches on Congress' judgments about which executive officers can and should enjoy a degree of independence from Presidential removal, and it cannot be squared with <em>Seila Law</em>, which relied extensively on such agency comparisons.<em>  </em>Not only did <em>Seila Law </em>contrast the CFPB's powers against those of the 1935 FTC in <em>Humphrey's Executor</em>, see 591 U.&nbsp;S<em>.</em>, at ___-___ (slip op., at 16-17), as well as the independent counsel in <em>Morrison</em>, see 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 17-18), it concluded that the FHFA (along with the Comptroller of the Currency, the Office of Special Counsel, and the Social Security Administration) does not possess "regulatory or enforcement authority remotely comparable to that exercised by the CFPB."  <em>Id.</em>, at ___-___ (slip op., at 20-21).  Those distinctions underpinned <em>Seila Law</em>'s proclamation that the CFPB had "no basis in history and no place in our constitutional structure."  <em>Id.</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 18).  In the Court's view today, however, all of those comparisons were irrelevant to the bottom-line question whether the CFPB Director's tenure protections comport with the Constitution.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court today also suggests that whether an agency regulates private individuals or Government actors does not meaningfully affect the separation-of-powers analysis.  <em>Ante</em>, at 30-31 ("[T]he President's removal power serves important purposes regardless of whether the agency in question affects ordinary Americans by directly regulating them or by taking actions that have a profound but indirect effect on their lives").<em>  </em>That, too, is flatly inconsistent with <em>Seila Law</em>, which<em> </em>returned repeatedly to this consideration.  Not only did <em>Seila Law </em>distinguish the CFPB from the independent counsel in <em>Morrison </em>on this basis, see 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 18), it distinguished the CFPB from both the FHFA and the Office of Special Counsel for the same reason, see <em>id</em>., at ___ (slip op., at 20).  That the Court is unwilling to stick to the methodology it articulated just last Term in <em>Seila Law </em>is a telltale sign that the Court's  separation-of-powers jurisprudence has only continued to lose its way.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court has proved far too eager in recent years to insert itself into questions of agency structure best left to Congress.  In striking down the independence of the FHFA Director, the Court reaches further than ever before, refusing tenure protections to an Agency head who neither wields significant executive power nor regulates private individuals.  Troublingly, the Court justifies that result by ignoring the standards it set out just last Term in <em>Seila Law</em>.  Because I would afford Congress the freedom it has long possessed to make officers like the FHFA Director independent from Presidential control, I respectfully dissent.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The federal parties also argue that the Recovery Act's succession clause bars the shareholders' statutory claim.  Because we have concluded that the statutory claim is already barred by the anti-injunction clause, we do not address this argument.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Rop </em>v. <em>FHFA</em>, 485 F. Supp. 3d 900, 915 (WD Mich. 2020).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Wiener</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 357 U.&nbsp;S. 349 (1958), the Court read a removal restriction into the War Claims Act of 1948.  But it did so on the rationale that the War Claims Commission was an adjudicatory body, and as such, it had a unique need for "absolute freedom from Executive interference."  <em>Id.,</em> at 353, 355-356.  The FHFA is not an adjudicatory body, so <em>Shurtleff</em>, not <em>Weiner, </em>is the more applicable precedent<em>.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Amicus </em>argues that<em> </em>there is historical support for the removal restriction at issue here because the Comptroller of Currency and the members of the Sinking Fund Commission were subject to similar protection, but those agencies are materially different because neither of them operated beyond the President's control, and one of them was led by a multi-member Commission.  As we explained in <em>Seila Law</em>, with the exception of a 1-year aberration during the Civil War, the Comptroller was removable at will by the President, who needed only to communicate the reasons for his decision to Congress.  591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___, n.&nbsp;5 (slip op., at 19, n.&nbsp;5).  And the Sinking Fund Commission, which Congress created to purchase U. S. securities following the Revolutionary War, was run by a 5-member Commission, and three of those Commissioners were part of the President's Cabinet and therefore removable at will.  See An Act Making Provision for the Reduction of the Public Debt, ch. 47, 1 Stat. 186 (1790).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Amicus</em> claims that <em>O'Melveny &amp; Myers</em> v. <em>FDIC</em>, 512 U.&nbsp;S. 79 (1994), supports his argument, but that decision is far afield.  It held that state law, not federal common law, governed an attribute of the FDIC's status as receiver for an insolvent savings bank.  <em>Id.,</em> at 81-82.  The nature of the FDIC's authority in that capacity sheds no light on the nature of the FHFA's distinctive authority as conservator under the Recovery Act.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Amicus</em> warns that if the Court holds that the Recovery Act's removal restriction violates the Constitution, the decision will "call into question many other aspects of the Federal Government."  Brief for Court-Appointed <em>Amicus</em> <em>Curiae</em> 47.  <em>Amicus</em> points to the Social Security Administration, the Office of Special Counsel, the Comptroller, "multi-member agencies for which the chair is nominated by the President and confirmed by the Senate to a fixed term," and the Civil Service.  <em>Id.,</em> at 48 (emphasis deleted).  None of these agencies is before us, and we do not comment on the constitutionality of any removal restriction that applies to their officers.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In the alternative, they request that the dividend payments be "recharacteriz[ed] .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. as a pay down of the liquidation preference and a corresponding redemption of Treasury's Government Stock."  App. 118.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Settled precedent also confirms that the unlawfulness of the removal provision does not strip the Director of the power to undertake the other responsibilities of his office, including implementing the third amendment.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Seila Law</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 30-36).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p> What we said about standing in <em>Seila Law</em> should not be misunderstood as a holding on a party's entitlement to relief based on an unconstitutional removal restriction.  We held that a plaintiff that challenges a statutory restriction on the President's power to remove an executive officer can establish standing by showing that it was harmed by an action that was taken by such an officer and that the plaintiff alleges was void.  See 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 9-10).  But that holding on standing does not mean that actions taken by such an officer are void <em>ab initio</em> and must be undone.  Compare <em>post</em>, at 2 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch, J</small></span>., concurring in part).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p> In addition, the constitutional defect in <em>Bowsher</em> was different from the defect here.  In <em>Bowsher</em>, the Comptroller General, whom Congress had long viewed as "an officer of the Legislative Branch," 478 U.&nbsp;S., at 731, was vested with executive power.  Here, the FHFA Director is clearly an executive officer.  See <em>post,</em> at 5-6 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J.</small></span>, concurring).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The lower courts may also consider all issues related to the federal parties' argument that the doctrine of laches precludes any relief.  The federal parties argue that Treasury was prejudiced by the shareholders' delay in filing suit because, for some time after the third amendment was adopted, there was a chance that it would benefit the shareholders.  According to the federal parties, the shareholders waited to file suit until it became apparent that the third amendment would not have that effect.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The shareholders respond that laches is inapplicable because they filed their complaint within the time allowed by the statute of limitations, and they argue that their delay did not cause prejudice because it was "mathematically impossible" for Treasury to make less money under the Third Amendment than under the prior regime.  Reply Brief for Collins et&nbsp;al. 4-5 (emphasis deleted).  We decline to decide this fact-bound question in the first instance.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Another limit on the judicial power is relevant: A party seeking relief must have a legal right to redress.  See <em>Cohens </em>v. <em>Virginia</em>, 6 Wheat. 264, 405 (1821) (explaining that Article III "does not extend the judicial power to every violation of the constitution which may possibly take place").  The judicial power extends only "to 'a case in law or equity,' in which a right, under such law, is asserted."  <em>Ibid.  </em>We have indicated that individuals may have an implied private right of action under the Constitution to seek equitable relief to "&nbsp;'preven[t] entities from acting unconstitutionally.'&nbsp;"  <em>Free Enterprise Fund </em>v. <em>Public Company Accounting Oversight Bd.</em>, 561 U.&nbsp;S. 477, 491, n. 2 (2010).  This includes "Appointments Clause or separation-of-powers claim[s]."  <em>Ibid.</em>  I assume the shareholders have brought such a cause of action here and have a legal right to obtain equitable relief if they can show they suffered an injury traceable to a Government action that violates the Constitution.  The shareholders did not raise the Administrative Procedure Act (APA) in count four of their complaint, but now contend their "constitutional claim is cognizable under the APA," which permits a "&nbsp;'reviewing court [to] <em>hold unlawful and</em> <em>set aside</em> agency action  found to be  contrary to constitutional right, power, privilege, or immunity.'&nbsp;"  Brief for Collins et&nbsp;al. 74 (quoting 5 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 706; ellipses omitted; emphasis in original).  Even assuming they raised their constitutional claim under the APA, it would not change the analysis; the shareholders would need to show they suffered an injury traceable to a Government <em>action</em> that violates the Constitution.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Because the shareholders allege the Government acted unlawfully, because their alleged injury can be traced to those allegedly unlawful actions, and because this Court might be able to redress that injury, I agree with the Court that they have standing.  See <em>Steel Co. </em>v. <em>Citizens for Better Environment</em>, 523 U.&nbsp;S. 83, 89 (1998).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also <em>Stern </em>v. <em>Marshall</em>, 564 U.&nbsp;S. 462, 503 (2011) (bankruptcy judges, as Article I officers, cannot exercise exclusive Article III power); <em>Clinton </em>v. <em>City of New York</em>, 524 U.&nbsp;S. 417, 438-441, 448-449 (1998) (the President, an Article II officer, cannot exercise Article I line-item-veto power); <em>Morrison </em>v. <em>Olson</em>, 487 U.&nbsp;S. 654, 677-679 (1988) (a law cannot give a court powers that violated Article III);<em> Glidden Co. </em>v. <em>Zdanok</em>, 370 U.&nbsp;S. 530, 584 (1962) (plurality opinion) (concluding after exhaustive analysis that two courts were Article III courts); <em>id.,</em> at 585-588 (Clark, J., concurring in result) (agreeing "in light of the congressional power exercised and the jurisdiction enjoyed, together with the characteristics of its judges"); <em>American Ins. Co. </em>v. <em>356 Bales of Cotton</em>, 1 Pet. 511, 546 (1828) (a territorial court is an Article I court and admiralty jurisdiction  can be exercised only by Article III courts, but Article IV removes this limitation with respect to the Territories).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p> I agree with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span> that a court must look at more than the label to determine in what branch an officer sits.  <em>Post</em>, at 3, n.&nbsp;1 (opinion concurring in part).  To answer this question, courts have historically looked at various factors.  See n.3, <em>supra</em>.  Here, everything about the Director's position, except the removal restriction, indicates he is an executive officer.  See also <em>ante,</em> at 35, n. 25 (opinion of the Court).  As the Court correctly explains, "the removal restriction . . . violates the separation of powers" because the Director is an executive officer.  <em>Ante</em>, at 32.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Seila Law</em>, the Court did not address whether an officer acts unlawfully if protected by an unlawful removal restriction.  See <em>ante</em>, at 34, and nn.&nbsp;23-24.  That is because the Government in effect conceded the issue.  <em>Seila Law</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (plurality opinion) (slip op., at 30); <em>id.,</em> at ___ (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.) (slip op., at 17).  Perhaps we should have addressed it then.  <em>Post</em>, at 6-7, n.&nbsp;2 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, J.).  </p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I continue to adhere to the views that I expressed in <em>Seila Law</em>: A combination of statutes can produce a separation-of-powers violation that renders Government action unlawful.  See 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part) (slip op., at 21).  In remedying such a separation-of-powers violation, courts cannot purport to rewrite the statute to avoid the violation.  <em>Ibid.</em>; <em>post, </em>at 6, n.&nbsp;2 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, J.) ("[W]e cannot divine 'which of the provisions' Congress would have kept and which it would have scrapped . . . had it known its actual choice was unconstitutional," "absent statutory direction from Congress").  However, I respectfully part ways with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch,</small></span> because, on the merits, I am uncertain whether the unlawful removal restriction here combines with any other statutory provision in a way that renders the Government action at issue unlawful.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A removal restriction may unconstitutionally insulate an officer such that his actions are unlawful.  If the President tries to remove an official  but a court blocks this action, then that official is not lawfully occupying his office and would likely be acting without authority.  Cf. <em>ante</em>, at 35.  But that circumstance has not arisen here. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The APA might permit this type of lawsuit in allowing an individual to challenge an agency action as "arbitrary, capricious, an abuse of discretion, or otherwise not in accordance with law."  5 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 706(2)(A).  There is a colorable argument that a Government official's misunderstanding about the scope of the President's removal authority would render an agency action arbitrary or capricious in certain cases.  However, the shareholders did not bring this constitutional challenge as an arbitrary and capricious claim against the FHFA.  And if they had, we would need to consider the interaction between this statutory claim and the Act's anti-injunction provision.  Cf. <em>ante,</em> at 12-13.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court's attempt to sidestep these cases leads nowhere.  <em>Seila Law</em>, we are told, discussed standing--not remedies--when it said plaintiffs "&nbsp;'sustain[&nbsp;] injury'&nbsp;" from unlawfully insulated executive action and may "challeng[e] [such] action as void."  See <em>ante,</em> at 34, n.&nbsp;24.  But standing and remedies are joined at the hip:  Article III permits a court only to provide "a <em>remedy</em> that redresses the plaintiffs' <em>injury-in-fact</em>."  <em>Collins</em> v. <em>Mnuchin</em>, 938 F.&nbsp;3d 553, 609 (CA5 2019) (Oldham, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part) (emphasis added).  That is why a plaintiff "must have standing [for] each form of relief"<em> </em>sought.  <em>Town of Chester </em>v. <em>Laroe Estates, Inc.</em>, 581 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2017) (slip op., at 5).  <em>Bowsher</em>, we are told, involved a legislative officer--not an executive one, which supposedly makes all the difference.  <em>Ante</em>, at 35, n.&nbsp;25.  But there the Comptroller was legislative only in the sense that he headed an "independent" department and was accountable to Congress rather than the President.  478 U.&nbsp;S, at 730-732.  If there is any difference here, it's that the FHFA Director--who likewise heads an "independent" agency, 12 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 4511(a)--is accountable to <em>no one</em>. <em> </em>The idea that whether acts are void or not turns on a label rather than on the functions an officer is<em> </em>assigned and who he is accountable to should not be taken seriously.  <em>E.g.,</em> <em>Bowsher</em>, 478 U.&nbsp;S., at 727-728, 732-733; <em>Free Enterprise Fund </em>v. <em>Public Company Accounting Oversight Bd.</em>, 561 U.&nbsp;S. 477, 484-486, 496-498 (2010); <em>Seila Law</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___, ___ (slip op., at 4-6, 23); <em>ante</em>, at 27-29, 31-32.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> stakes out more foreign terrain.  After saying that he "join[s] the Court's opinion in full," he argues there was no constitutional violation at all because the President--despite statutes barring his way--was free to remove the Director all along.  <em>Ante</em>, at 1, 4, 11.  Accordingly, it seems <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> disagrees with all of Part III-B's merits analysis in addition to the Court's novel remedy in Part III-C.  Like the Court, though, he seemingly takes as given that Congress would have chosen to adopt HERA even if it had known this course required subjecting the Director to removal by the President.  <em>Ante</em>, at 5-6.  In doing so, he parts ways with his opinion last year in <em>Seila Law</em>, where he recognized the following:  First, in cases like ours, a constitutional violation arises because of "the combination" of statutory terms that (1) confer executive power on an official and (2) improperly insulate him from removal.  591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part) (slip op., at 21).  Second, absent statutory direction from Congress, we cannot divine "which of the provisions" Congress would have kept and which it would have scrapped--or what else it might have done--had it known its actual choice was unconstitutional.  <em>Id.,</em> at ___ (slip op., at 23).  Third, this Court lacks the "&nbsp;'editorial freedom'&nbsp;" to pick and choose among options like these, for doing so would usurp Congress's legislative authority.  <em>Ibid.  </em>Today, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> suggests <em>Seila Law</em> rested on one party's concession about the meaning of the law.  <em>Ante</em>, at 8, n.&nbsp;5; <em>ante</em>, at 10.  But parties cannot stipulate to the law.  <em>E.g.,</em> <em>Zivotofsky </em>v. <em>Kerry</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 41, n.&nbsp;2 (2015) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); <em>Young </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 315 U.&nbsp;S. 257, 258-259 (1942).  More importantly, his observations were right then--and they remain so today.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I join Parts I and II of the Court's opinion rejecting petitioners' argument that the FHFA actions under review violated the Housing and Economic Recovery Act of 2008, as well as Part III-C discussing what the appropriate remedial implications would be if the FHFA Director's for-cause removal protection were unconstitutional.  I join also Part II of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s concurrence concerning the proper remedial analysis for the Fifth Circuit to conduct on remand.  Finally, I note that <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span>' arguments that an improper removal restriction does not necessarily render agency action unlawful warrant further consideration in an appropriate case.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> explained in dissent, <em>Seila Law </em>rested on implausible recharacterizations of this Court's separation-of-powers jurisprudence.  I continue to believe that <em>Seila Law</em> was wrongly decided.  Whatever the merits of that decision, however, it does not support invalidating the FHFA Director's independence.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;By comparison, the CFPB has fined private actors billions of dollars.  <em>Seila Law LLC </em>v.<em> Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2020) (slip op., at 5).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The GSEs' ongoing ties with the Government long fueled public perception that the Government would intervene if the GSEs were in danger of collapse.  See Congressional Research Serv., Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac in Conservatorship: Frequently Asked Questions 2 (updated May 31, 2019) (noting that it was "widely believed prior to 2008 that the federal government was an implicit backstop for the GSEs in light of their congressional charters").  This perception became reality during the 2008 financial crisis, when the Treasury Department extended hundreds of billions of dollars in credit to Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, and the FHFA put those entities under conservatorship.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

<section class="lawyer-search-section section"><div class="lawyer-search-section__body lawyer-search-section__body--only-search">
    <div class="lawyer-search-section__search">
        
    <div class="lawyer-search">
    <h2 class="lawyer-search__search-title">Need to find an attorney?</h2>
    <p class="lawyer-search__search-subtitle">Search our directory by legal issue</p>
    <p class="lawyer-search__form-instructions">Enter information in one or both fields (Required)</p>
    <form class="lawyer-search__form" method="get" action="https://lawyers.findlaw.com/lawyer/lawyer_dir/search/jsp/stdSearch_process.jsp">
        <div class="lawyer-search__input-group lawyer-search__input-group--legal-issue">

    <script src="//caselaw.findlaw.com/static/fi/images/etc/designs/flcommon/flfe/javascript/lp.redesign.js"></script><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        var pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if(practiceCode)
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }

        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {

        });

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}
